O, sons, because I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, by its completion the end is achieved in it, and by its expositions, and by its declarations, it is my view that you complete this for you with this treatise, which is a part of the operation with my hand, so it is my surgery.
Because the operation with my hand is prostrate in our region and in our time entirely deprived, whereas many perils the science of it, and its vestiges are held hostage; and there remain of it only some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have been muddled and become subject to those errors and uncertainty because their clauses the understandings of it and is elongated the force of it and the art.
And it is seen to me that you bring that to life with the order of this treatise in that according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and for that it comes with the forms of the tools of cautery and of the other instruments of the work as it may be by addition of the declaration, and by preparation of it, which cause is necessary.
But the cause for which no one is found a good artificer with his hand in this our time, is because the art of medicine is long, and that it requires the actor to be beforehand exercised in the science of anatomy, which reconciles Gualia because it knows the movements of the members and the forms of those, and the conjunction and the separation of them; and had the knowledge of the bones, of the nerves and of the cartilages, and the number of those, and the growth of them; and of the pulsating veins and of the quiet ones, and of the places of the emergence of them.
And for that it says Hippocrates that with a name it is not moved, but in the work little, and most of all in the art of the hand.
But we have already said of that in the introduction of this book; because whoever is not learned in what we have said of anatomy, is not evacuated that he does not fall into error for which many men die, thus as I have seen too many of those, because some are formed in this science and cast themselves from that their science and their experience.
Because I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician who had incised an abscess scrophilos in the neck of a woman, and incised some arteries of the neck so that great blood flowed while the woman died among the hands of him.
And I saw another physician who thought that the stomach of a man who had been in his state should be treated with a stone, and the stone was large, and it came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, for that man on the third day died.
And I had already been called to extract that, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the disposition of the patient I knew over him that he would die.
And I saw another physician who was taking a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and he was to a black man who was broken in his hip; and prepared the physician with his ignorance, extracted the fracture, over the wound, with splints and splints with strong structure, and did not leave the wound to breathe.
And then he dismissed him according to his desires, and he relieved him, and he left him for some days and commanded that we untie the ligament; and so it was that the hip and the foot became infected, and it came to perdition.
And I was called to him, and I arranged to untie the ligament, and achieved tranquility and so he was relieved of his pains.
But however the corruption was already confirmed in the limb, for that I could not restrain it, and did not cease to wander the corruption in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated after some days, until the malignancy of it was magnified upon his lord.
The cause of which is that the cancer is pure of melancholic humor, therefore it does not come to it with iron at all, but that it is in a limb which suffers that it be entirely held.
And therefore, sons, it is necessary to you that the operation with the hand be devised in two divisions: that is in the operation to which is associated health, and in the work with which there is peril in the major dispositions.
And I have already explained this in all places of this book in which I bring operation in which there is error and fear; for it is necessary to you that you guard this and leave that, so that the foolish do not find a way to speak and to dishonor you.
And therefore take your arms with diligence and with the protection of God, and your patients with happiness and with firmness.
And use the best way by the grace of God, pursuing health and praiseworthy success, and leave the maladies terrible of difficult healing, and hold your arms from that which makes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not escape you: because it is remaining to your glory, and more so in the world and later to your sanctities.
Gualia already said in some of his monitions: "Do not treat a bad illness, so that you are not called bad physicians."
And now I have divided this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and the first of the first part. The first chapter, ordered from the head to the feet, in the forms of the instruments and in the tools of the cauteries, and of that which is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of incision and perforation, and of leeches, and of poultices, and of the extraction of arrows and of things similar to those, all chaptered and ordered by the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the cure of sprains and of things similar to those; chapter ordered from the head to the feet, and of the forms of the instruments.
I want you to say the quality of the judgments of that cautery and of its consequences, and in what completion cautery should be prohibited.
Therefore I say that the sermon of the quality of the judgment of cautery and of its consequences is a long and prolix sermon, and subtle by science, and secretly hidden; and already many wise men have spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and to me a little of their sermons suffices, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery commends itself to every complexion universally, which is in matter or has matter, except for two complexions, which are complexions with hot matter.
In the cold and dry complexion with matter, there is disagreement; because some say that cautery is beneficial to that, and others say the contrary of that, that cautery is not convenient in illness which is by quality and by dryness, because the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that illness cold and dry with that which is hot and dry be healed.
And they say those who say the contrary of that, that cautery is made beneficial in all cold and dry illness that comes in the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of man and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say for the sermon of that, that experience has already to me some times discovered.
But however, it is not necessary that it should come upon this that which is already exploited and exercised in the chapter of cautery by previous exercise, and who has known the diversity of the complexion of men and the disposition of the illnesses in their own, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the space of time of those.
But in the other complexions, there is no fear for you, and most of all in the hot and humid illnesses, because all the physicians are agreed upon those, and they are not disagreed in the judgment with cautery in those.
And know, sons, that of the secret of healing by cautery with fire and of the goodness of it, it is because fire is simple, the action of which does not reach beyond the member which is cauterized and does not reach the other member attached to it except with little consequence.
But the action of burning medicine reaches that in the members which are far from it, and greatly makes them come to the member illness of difficult healing and greatly to be avoided.
But fire, by its sublimity and of its substance goodness, does not do this except that it becomes superfluous, and it is already known to us by experiment and by the length of service of study in the art about the truth of the causes; and for that I am excused from the length of the sermon, and if it were not that it is not read in my book in this pause to you secretly hidden with fire, and the quality of its operation in the bodies and of its expulsion by which it expels the illnesses, with a philosophical sermon demonstrative, which would be acute in your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree about the time in which it is convenient to do cautery and pause the best time of spring.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because so that the consequence for the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which with the cautery is thrown, and most of all if the cautery is for pains or of necessary pains, strong and fistulous, which do not suffer delay because it is feared of those that come malice, which is greater than little consequence entering for time.
And thus do not catch in your minds, or, sons, that this is not estimated except by the vulgar people and the foolish physicians, because it is that to that illness which is healed by cautery is not reduced at all times, and they pause that consequence.
And the cause is not so as they think about it, because cautery is nothing but in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause for which the pains come.
But however, cautery superflues over medicine by the will of its validity and virtue of its operation and greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that its illness returns at some time in the future, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of its illness, and the virtue of it, and what is prepared in the body of it from the aggregation of superfluities in it, and what has come to it in its acquisition in food and things similar to those; unless that illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a member of few superfluities and humors, as cautery of a tooth for pain and things similar to that, it is possible that in it it does not return that pain, but rather that it is done in small.
And more common speech is that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is a right speech, not that I understand it in it, because I think that it is no cure that profits with medicine nor with any other cause except cautery.
And the cause is the opposite of that; and the understanding that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is only when they administer the methods of cure in some diseases, and those medicines do not confer, and then at the end of the cause one administers cautery, and it profits to that. And therefore it is said that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding that the common people and too many foolish doctors understand.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say that except for the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not make a scar, as it is not absolutely true, because I have already seen that and found that it does not do that except in some bodies, and cautery with that is good and better than with iron as they said; but however when you do cautery with fire with gold, it is not declared to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity that you want for the roar of gold, and that prepares to receive heat; and if you adjust on it fire it melts the gold by the fire, and happens in the fire some plates for that the artisan catches for the occupation of that.
And for that reason cautery with iron is done more lightly and more directly to the rectitude of operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter in cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those of the head within the feet, so that later the one who asks for it will know what he wants from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization confers on the humidity and coldness over the nerves affected by the accident of which there are two causes, so it is the cause of soda and multitude of rheumatic flux, called corissa, from the head to the parts of the ears and of the ears flowing, and multitude of sleeping, and of toothache and of the throat, and universally of all disease that comes from coldness, as well as paralytically, soda and apoplexy, and similar in those diseases.
The form of this cauterization is this, that first you command the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine the head modifying, for three or for four nights, according to what requires the virtue of the patient and the age of him and his custom.
And then command him to shave his head, and then make that place between your hands on a padded bench and that he place his hands on his feet.
And then place the razor of your palm near the nose between his ears. But however, as your saying of the middle place has arrived, sign that place with a tent, and then ignite the cautery olive of which this is the form:
And then place that on the place marked with a tent, in the manner that it descends, with which your hand be constricted little by little, and you turn that.
And then lift your hand slightly and look at the place, and if you see already of the bone being discovered some quantity of the head, as well as the quantity of grain of ears, then elevate your hand, and if not turn your hand with that iron, with another of that, if it is cooled, until it is seen of the bone that I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve that on the place and leave that for three days.
And then place on it a cloth soaked in butter and leave that on it until the scar of the fire is destroyed. And then cure the place with tetrapharmacon ointment until it is healed.
And already they said that when the plague remains open, so much better gets the scar, because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them said that it should be cauterized the bone inside the bone, and that the cautery should be kept until some part of the surface of the bone is burned.
And then it is scraped that which is burned of the bone, and then it is cured.
And others said that with the cautery it should reach the end, until it presses on the bone with force until the bone breaks, and this is the form of alphecati small: And they estimate that from that place expire the vapors of the head.
And it should be kept open for a long time, and then it should be cured until it is consolidated.
But to me, these two kinds of cautery have not been seen at all, except in some men according to their way of fear; and leaving that, to me, it is better for health, when it is; because the head is revealed by the solution of continuity of it as well as we have testified in the other members, and more so when the head of the patient is of cold nature.
And the species of cautery first is more safe and better to me; and from that I use and with that work for health.
Second chapter.
Of the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs throughout the head and it is prolonged in the patient, and use of herbs, and poultices, and purgatives, and plasters, and guats, and cadels, and gualinas, and semblances, and most importantly if it has already been cauterized with a cautery that is recounted and does not benefit him from any of those causes, then consider that if the head of the patient is of strong creation in its nature; and is not frail and finds a lot of great friction, then cauterize him with another cautery besides that one a little. Then cauterize that one on the horn of his head with a cautery while the code is parted. Incise the code and uncover the quantity of bone which we have recounted and cauterize that with a cautery in the back part of the head in the place which is called vas, — and that is the place in which a cupping glass is placed. And alleviate your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, because the patient by this finds a great deal of pain different from the pain of all the other cauteries of the head.
And you say this cautery in its place, because whoever cauterizes with the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is the form of it.
Chapter Three.
Of cauterization of a non-ancient hernia.
When pain in the head is felt, or pain extends into the eye, it should be evacuated with medicines that modify the head, and another cure should be administered, which I have said in the divisions of the illnesses; and if none of these are helpful, then cauterization in it is in two ways: either cauterization with acute burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with burning medicine is that you take a piece of alum, and you cut it and remove the extremities of it from both ends. And then incise the place of pain in the temple with a lancet, as long as it is in that place, after the bone, while it is hidden.
And then strengthen over it with a stopper by descending constriction, and leave it for the space of three hours; and then untie it and remove the alum, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then place over it a poultice submerged in butter until the place heals.
And then cure this with gargles until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written in the two treatises of burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron according to this manner: ignite a cautery of which this is the form:
and it is called a "claus clavilhs," because the head of it is as shaped as a clove in which there is some curvature, and in the middle part of it is a small eminence.
And then place over the place of pain, and keep your hand — so that the artery which is below is not burned — and revolve the iron little by little with speed.
And if the quantity of it that is burned by the specificity of the bone is as much as most of it, and elevate your hand so that the artery below is not burned, so that there does not come a flow of blood.
And then imbibe a poultice in salt water and place it over the place, and leave it for three days; and then place a poultice crushed with butter, and then cure with gargles until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the extremity of the scalpel according to the cautery, and guard yourself from incision of the artery in this hernia properly non-ancient.
Chapter Four.
Of cauterization of an ancient hernia.
When you cure a hernia with what we have said of the previous cure, and what we have said in the divisions of the illnesses, and if none of the cures are helpful, and sometimes because of the illness that does not suffice to him what we have said of the first cauterization with medicine or the cauterization with fire, then you must ignite the scalpel cautery while it is white-hot, because you signify over the place of pain with a line of length of which the majority of it, or which that one; and then pause your hand for one moment and strengthen it so that you may incise the artery and reach into the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to guard against the continuation of the jawbone, which is bad for chewing, and that the nerve which is bad for it is not burned, and that it does not become a spasm.
And if it is about the custody and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, because in the incision of it there is fear, and especially to him who ignores what is done and is not studious and experienced; and leave the operation to him at the beginning.
And I will come with a remembrance of the regimen of the flow of blood accidentally from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see of this illness that this cauterization does not suffice and you see the body of the patient tolerable, cauterize that in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and cure the wound until it is healed.
And if you, administer the cautery which we have said in the chapter of cauterizing the artery, with a cautery having two handles, and this cauterization is better and more convenient.
Chapter V.
Of cauterization of ear pains.
When in the ear there is pain due to inflammation, and it is cured with laxative causes and with the other cure which I have said in the division, and the pain is not removed, then ignite the cautery, and this is the one which has a point, of which this is the form:
And when it is pointed, ignite it, and it is moved around the entire ear like a wheel is turned, or in the circuit of each part if the pain is in each part. Lengthen it with the cautery from the root of the ear little by little, so that you feel the location with the tip.
And if the cautery of ten points in the whole ear, and which one, and then cure the places while they are healing.
Chapter VI.
Of cauterization of a tumor.
A tumor which is cured and by cauterization is only of the kinds which come by phlegm, according to what we have said in the divisions of the diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the kind which arises from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you cure that kind of tumor with herbs and purgatives and washings, and if you do not find comfort in your cure, then you must cauterize the tumor with three cauteries: the first at the root of the ear, the second a little from its temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And pause your cautery for the opposite of the diseased part, because softening does not come except in the part that is healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize that with the cautery in the right of the extremity of the ear, a little above the horn of the head, and in the temple another, the length of which should be according to the length of the finger, and pause your hand with the cautery until the quantity of the majority of the species of the cord is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a species of the cutlery, the form of which is shown opposite. But however more subtle is that one a little as you see; and that handle in its place of superfluous thickness a little.
And then cure the place with the water that is opposite, produced until it is healed.
Chapter VII.
Of cauterization of old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is old and you cure that with the water which we have said and if you do not find comfort in your medication and the malady has no fever, then cauterize that with four cauteries: on top of all the horns of its head; and with cautery and the middle location of the head, as we have said; and with cautery in the nape of the neck. And the manner of the cautery is made according to what has been said before.
And it is already cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; and it is more behind; and then it is cured with the water that proceeds.
Chapter VIII.
Of cauterization of oblivion made by flame.
When the patient is given at the beginning large herbs and pills of the serving modifying.
And then shave the head of the patient completely and place on the nape of his neck a plaster of cente described in the treatise of plasters.
And pause that some times, because that is the manner of cauterizing. And do water according to that same order which I have already said.
And therefore if by those he is healed, well; and if not, then make to him three cauteries in the rear part of his head, which are descending from the most superior part of the head into the part more below the neck. And pause between each cautery the thickness of the finger, and then cure the cautery with the water that proceeds.
But if you add more and the patient is water tolerant, then make to him a cautery in the middle location; and if you add more, then cauterize that above the two horns.
And then cure that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive according to the form previously given.
Chapter IX.
Of cauterization of paralysis and of softening of the whole body.
When you process in softening the head with herbs and with the water which we have said; and then shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize that with a cautery in the middle location of the head, and with a cautery above all the horns of the head, and with a cautery above the rear part of it, and with three cauteries above the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the malady of softening of the body of too much that and the maladies tolerant and the malady is strong, then cauterize that with four cauteries above the vertebrae of the loins, and certainly with a cautery until it is burned of the cord of it, and elevate your hand. And then carefully treat the one who is being healed, and this cautery olive.
Chapter X.
Of cauterization of epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized, unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore, if you modify the brain of him at the beginning with years and with the other cure which we have said in the division when the patient is great and tolerates the excess of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then administer guarguarsmes and masticators to the servant, before that for a few days, with the blessing of the food.
And then shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize him with a cautery in the middle location of the head according to the previous manner, and with another cautery in the nape of his neck, and on each horn of his head cauterize. And if the patient is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with cauteries as I have said, having paralysis and softening of the body on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And be cauteries olive according to what has preceded.
But if the patient is a child, then pause cauteries subtle according to this manner.
Chapter XI.
Of cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are humidities corrupted and phlegm gross, then cauterize him with the cauteries which we have said in the having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is superfluity declining to melancholy, and the body of the patient is humid, then give him to drink water that purifies the brain of him according to what has preceded in the division.
And then shave the head of the patient, and then make a decent tortora of him over the circle, and then place that in the middle location of the head.
And the patient be sensed cauterized, and be held from all parts.
And then pause liniment; and it is more worthy of bordering with wool of sheep. And warm that over the fire to temperate calefaction by measure, which the said suffers when it is most in him. And then put that in the middle location of the head of him in the circle, and let him enter that it be enfregidated.
And make water with the patient every week once, with the other regimen of him good as long as he is healed; or if you will, cauterize this by making small points according to the best of him, unless you have the hand with a cautery thus arranged: this is a species of cautery which wets the brain with equality.
And then pause that cot in wool or in gray of guinea.
Chapter XII.
Of cauterization of water descending in the heel.
When to you is declared the beginning of water in the heel by the signs which I have said in the division, then begin and give to the patient to drink water that purifies the head of him, and guard that from all humidities.
And make that suzar in bath in degu for some days.
And then command him to shave his head, and cauterize that with a cautery in the middle location of the head; and then cauterize that with two cauteries on two temples if it is the beginning of ysement of water in two heels, or of one latz if it is the beginning of the water in one heel.
Incide with cautery all the veins and arteries which are left the codena; and be the cauteries in that length in the latitude of the temples.
And guard yourself from fluxes of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see of him fluxes of blood, stop him with some medicine, which is to you possible.
And you will come with wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with abscess of those and caution of fluxes of blood.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the cervix below two bones with two cauteries deliberately produced.
Chapter XIII.
Of cauterization of old tears.
When tears of the heels are old and by the veins and arteries which are in the manifest location of the head outside and it is verified that this is superfluity frigid phlegmatic, then cauterize that with a cautery which we have recounted in the chapter of the beginning of water falling in the heels, and with a cautery in the middle location of the head and with two cauteries in the cervix below two temples, and with two cauteries in the cervix below two bones. And if you need of addition, then cauterize that in all latz of the tail of the heel on the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter XIV.
Of cauterization of the stench of the nose.
When you medicate him with the water which we have said in the division and it does not profit your medication, then you equip and give to the patient the cochias to drink for three nights; and then shave the head of him, and cauterize him with a cautery middle with a cautery olive. And then cauterize that with a pointed cautery with two cauteries on the two eyebrows above the hairs a little.
And guard yourself from the arc that you do not burn that.
And of the pointed cautery this is the form.
Chapter .xv.
Of cautery of softening of eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened by illness or by humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery with this lunar cautery; and this is the form of it:
And if you, cauterize that above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries in every part, and they be elongated from the temples. And be the length of each cautery according to the quantity of the eyebrow.
And do not press too your hand with the cautery, but according to the quantity that the third part of the eyelid be cauterized.
Chapter .xvi.
Of cautery of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are converted to the parts of it and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are converted to the parts of it so that they prick the eye, cauterize in those in two manners, whether cauterize or is with burning medicine.
If with cautery of it with fire, command the patient in front of it that he see his hairs so as those which they clean, until they be prolonged and equal; and if prick that you must their naisment, then you strengthen the eyes of it with a bandage so that it be not bad until the hairs be born.
Therefore, as soon as they be born and equal, then place the head of the patient in your lap, and then send upon the eyelid of the eyes of it with tent, with a sign which be according to the figure of a leaf of myrtle, and be the beginning of the sign near the hairs.
And then place coto enclosed in album of hen or in mucilage of ptilli upon the eye.
And then ignite the cautery of which this is the form of it.
And then cauterize upon the figure which you have sended, little by little, in many times, until be burned the surface of the code which is as fuella of myrtle, all apparent properly.
And sign of health of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already be contracted and the hairs of it be elevated above the prick of the eye.
And therefore elevate your hand and leave it for three days, and then place upon it coto with border until be eradicated the scar. And then cure it with enguent until it be healed.
But if returns for the hairs any cause to their naisment and the eyelid is softened, then do another time the cautery upon that all as you had done first.
But if the hairs be in the eyelid below, then cauterize it until it be elevated to its natural place and be equal and that the hairs do not prick the eye.
But the cautery with burning medicine this is that you command the patient that he see the hairs until they be prolonged and equal, and then be made of cloth the form of a myrtle leaf.
And then take of known soap and of living cautery one. .i., or that, and then mix those causes well, and make it with festinacio so that it not be frozen; and then expand of it upon the cloth which is made as the form of a myrtle leaf, and place it upon one eye or both; and place below the eye coto enclosed in album of hen, and the head of the patient be in your lap.
And place the said upon the medicine and press it a little and not the bad until the patient feels the biting of the medicine, for biting is in it as of fire.
And therefore as perseveres it that the throbbing biting, then leave the medicine and wipe it with your hand; therefore as ceases the biting, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then consider if times the eyelid already is lifted as it be washed above the constriction with fire or with incision, for then it is well; and if not returns upon it of the medicine upon the place in which it has not put the medicine and do not degrade until be equal your operation and be contracted the eye.
And then place upon it coto with border until be eradicated the scar of the burning, and then cure it with enguent deafinicon or with another until it be healed.
And it is necessary to you, above your operation that you guard with pervigil diligence that in the eye does not fall re of the medicine, and if the eyelid apropos some days is softened and any cause of the hairs prick in the eye, then iterate your medicine upon that place the which of the eyelid is softened, and properly as you have done at the beginning, and then environ it until it be healed.
And this is the form of myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to the magnitude and the smallness, and therefore according to that is made your constriction.
And no one should be hidden the path of rectum to whom it is studied in this art.
Chapter XVII.
Of cauterization of fistula that is in the lacrimal of the heel.
When you cure a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of the diseases and do not confer your cure, then you must cauterize it according to this manner: command the patient to place his head on your knee, and hold the head of him some minister between your hands in such a manner that it is not harmed nor agitated with his head.
And then place a cloth soaked in oil or in a poultice of ptilli on the heel of him.
And then ignite the cautery of which this is the form:
Which is concave, as is the form of a reed or the feather of a vulture, from one extremity of which the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it should penetrate into the other extremity. And if you wish, it should be shaped as a tent.
But however, the one that is concave is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out of it pus and dry it out. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
And then place on it the cautery which is well ignited, and hold it with your hand a little distance from the cautery of the heel to the part of the nose, so that it does not burn your hand or the patient be agitated, and then place the cautery in the grains of the heel and corrupt it.
And therefore if at the beginning of your cauterization it penetrates into the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if it is necessary; and leave it so for three days, and then place on it a cloth with border and surround it with an astringent enguent until it is healed.
But if after eleven days it is not healed, then place on it a sharp corrosive medicine until the bone is uncovered and scraped according to what you have ordained in your chapter.
And there is another way of cauterizing a fistula which some first said: enter at the place of the fistula and end it and then place in that fissure a fine thread of which is the form: And melt in that the quantity of one ounce of lead; and hold your hand with the thread with good retention, and do not harm the patient so that the melted lead does not reach the heel of him; and when you place on the heel of the patient a cloth soaked in oil or in water, because lead burns the place of the fistula and heals it by a wonderful healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cauterization and the cure, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a traction from the nose and reduce the fistula to the course of the nose according to what will come in its place properly.

Chapter XX.
Of cauterization of the teeth and of softening of the gums.

Chapter XXI.
Of cauterizing tooth pain.
When tooth pain is due to cold, or a worm is in it, or it does not respond to treatment with medicine, then cauterization is done in that tooth in two ways: cauterization with a cow's horn, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with horn is that you take a piece of cow's horn and heat it in a blacksmith's forge, and then take a stick and wrap it around the tip of the horn, and then immerse that in the heated horn, and place it on the painful tooth firmly, until it is seared.
And then repeat that several times until the force of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or a stick in the heated horn below, and place it on the aching tooth, and place a heated iron on top until the iron reaches the bottom of the cavity of the tooth.
But however, cauterization with fire is that you attend to the handle of the iron or of the fire, and that it be in the center of it some thickness and the sharpness of the fire does not reach the mouth of the patient.
And then ignite the cauterizer, the shape of which will come, and place it on the tooth and hold your hand over it until the cauterizer is seared; and do this several times because the pain will be relieved either on that day or the next day.
And cover that with the impression of that cauterizer, the patient rinses his mouth with good water and holds it for an hour, then spits it out; and this is the shape of the cauterizer.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, according to what is possible for you.
And this is the shape of the cannula:

And then cure that with gargles and poultices and bandages until it is healed.

Chapter XLIII.
Of cauterization at the beginning of redness.

Of the cure of alconati.
This illness that is called alconati is nothing else but the one that is adjusted in the wound resembling water descending and it is not that one.
But the manner of acting in it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight saddle, and then take his head in your two hands and hold it so that you see the healing arrive at the lower parts, and then let it dry and not descend, and keep the light, and the patient sees the causes as he sees.
And if it does not descend below, you know that it is water.
But if the descent to the healing with water that we have said is not prepared, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a fine spatula and finish it on that over the panniculus, cutting a little of the continuation of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the crown (in the place where it is hollow), until the healing is removed.
Therefore, when it is removed, then you distill into the wound water cauda in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have already boiled fennel and honey.
And then cure it with the other causes until it is healed.
Chapter XXII.
Of the cure of the water descending from the wound.
We have already said the kinds of water in the division and which one it is convenient to perforate with a sermon, then this one according to the healing.
Then you must make the patient sit between your hands on a chair and tie his wound, tightening it.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of his wound if the wound in which is the water is the left; or on your right hand if the wound is the right.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the wound is left; or with your left hand, if the wound is right.
And then place the end of the almond near the crown for the thickness of the ray in the album of the wound from the side of the lacrimal gland, and then press the almond with virtue and roll your hand on it.
And then penetrate into the album of the wound and feel that the almond has already arrived at the visible cause.
And it is the measure of the path of the almond to the bottom the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, and it is the crown of the wound; because you will see with that pupil the vision of the wound through the clarity of the cornea.
And then place the almond into the place where is the water, and then you throw it down once, then again, and therefore if the water descends immediately, then immediately wet the patient's wound with water over which he has his sight, and almond in his wound.
And then rest for a while; therefore if the water has descended, then you remove that other time as long as the almond is not thrown away.
And therefore when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and roll it with your hand little by little, and then dissolve in the water a little clear salt of gemma and with that wash the wound inside; and then place outside the wound cotton or stopper or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white and lick the wound with water.
But not those who are of our time place on that comi trissat with egg white.
And if you do not obey your almond to the entrance of the wound because of its hardness — because it is some of the men whose wound is very hard — then you must take the spatula which is called alberid of which this is the form:
And therefore pierce with that that conjuncture, and do not end your hand in the track, and it is not unless you make a way to the almond place subtle.
And then introduce the almond according to what we have said.
And you must complete the work, prepare the patient to sleep comfortably in a dark room, where he sleeps on his back and is prohibited from all movements.
And keep him in nature so that his nature is cleansed, and do not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And bind him according to his disposition on the third day.
And after that in that dark room, remove the bandage and expose the foot to view, and have him look at some objects, and then return the bandage on the seventh day.
And do not let him do this at the time of the cure or soon after the infusion. Have it that you abstain from this because the water may leak lightly through the great and vehement regard.
But if an abscess occurs, then you must unbind the wound before the seventh day, and rectify it with water that flows into the abscess.
Then cover the sight of him and place a cloth on his face through which his breath may be regulated, leaving him in the dark room for some days.
And then gradually take him out of that room and converse with him about his affairs.
And know that in alchemy the disciple is not excused from vision on some occasions, and then you should be bold about the operation.
And we have now come to some of those who are in Artach, and some said that they make fire in the Ayrac magdaam pierced with which the water is sucked.
But however, I have not seen any in our land who had perfect water, nor is water mentioned in some of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is novel.
And these are the forms of the species of magdaam so that it may be on those who ignore those:
And they are made of iron or properly of iron, and their extremities are with this triangular subtlety, the acute extremity as such is the form.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of a wound in the nose.
When in the nose are born various fleshy growths, some of which are a cause that is assembled like a scorpion of moths' weight, or a polyp of weight; and this malady is called polyp because it resembles that weight represented. And of this is cancerous flesh, a fleshy, of dusky color; and of this is the flesh lion not of dusky color.
Therefore, in that which is in these fleshy lions not fraudulent nor cancerous, you must have the patient lie between your hands and placed in the sun, and breathe the nostrils of him, and prick your own finger on those fleshy growths. And then extract that to the outer parts, and then incisi the water that you have comprehended of those with a sharp, subtle spatula on one side, so that you know that all the flesh is already gone; if any cause of it remains, of which the abscission is possible, then you root that water with one of the subtle instruments with ease so that none of it remains. But if it is lordly with blood or an abscess occurs, then you pause to him that which you must; or is of the malignant abscesses, then you cauterize and cauterize that so that it is abscised blood and all the cavities are gone.
And then be gotten in the nose after the incision vi agre and water or wine. But if the nose is open and from it proceeds humidity into the palate, then you know that it is already healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate according to what you must, then you know that from within him is flesh wound in the pus that soars bones of the nose to which the abscission has not reached.
Therefore, then you must enter a thread of him to whom there is some thickness, and sew in him moth nuts, and place between each nut the quantity of the finger or less, and engage the patient and enter one extremity of the thread in the nose with the ray or with another cause which is to him possible.
And since that has been done, that he throws his scent so that it reaches the canals of the nose, and be spit on his throat, because troops of occasions the semblance to this action do the children in the schools, and is lighter cause to him who wants to do it.
And then place the two extremities of the thread, one extremity that exits over the nose and the other that remains in the nose, and then administer a suture of flesh with the nuts that are in the thread, and do this so that you know that the flesh is already with the nuts of the thread.
And then extract the thread, and place in the nose after the abstraction of the blood a poultice, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that is remaining of the flesh.
And at the end of the cause, place in the nose a lead tube for some days until it is healed.
But if you are in need of a cure that requires it, use that.
And this is the form of the instrument a capipurge maker with which oils and medicines are distilled in the nose:
And make a vessel of silver or lead, which is like a small open lamp, so like a small basin, and the meat seemingly.
And if you wish, make a closed tube like a cane, and the tube of the instrument to purge should be made flat and uncovered; to which there is a handle at the back of it, with which you hold it when you heat it in oil or water that you want of the juices and suckable causes.
Chapter XXV.
Of the nostrils in the extremities of the nose.
Many times the nostril in the extremities of the nose is inflamed, and it is magnified and mentioned in some days within which it is not possible to see the regard of it.
And for that reason, you should investigate that at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of that. And then place on the area a cautery or medicine that burns, which is in the place of it.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened, rough, of dark color and of little feeling, then do not touch that with iron because it is a foul abscess; because many times, to the one who incisions this abscess, a great evil comes upon the one who had it.
And if the abscess is of a light touch and not of dark color, and sometimes that incision is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient and incisions that without fear, and cure the area with water that exhales and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Of the suture of the nose and of the lips and of the ear when the continuity is dissolved by plague or by resemblances.
Know that when an accident occurs in the solution of continuity in one of these cartilages, little confidence is given in that operation unless in some men.
Therefore, when it is necessary to have some cause of those, consider if it is fresh plague with its blood, and then you adjust the two lips of the plague with suture, and then cure that until it is healed.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each one is made healthy, then you should scrape each part of its apparent code until blood comes out. And then adjust the two lips with suture and strengthen those, and over those pulverize blood of dragon and incense, and place over the powder a piece of diaphana plaster, or of some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave that tied for two days or three, and then untie that and replace that plaster and leave that until the threads are by themselves abscinditz, and then cure with an enguent until it is healed.
But the manner of the suture is that you add the solution of continuity or with needles as we have recommended in the suture of the belly, or add the solution of continuity with thread as you have done and have known.
Chapter XXVII.
Of the extraction of those causes that come in the two lips in the parts of the teeth.
Many times it happens to many men in the parts of the teeth of their lips some hard abscesses resembling fat of worms, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, you should convert the lips and fends each of those hardnesses from all parts, and then the abscess incision, fill the location with incense blood until the blood is restrained, and then be washed with vinegar and be cured the location with water that is styptic, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Of incision of the flesh adjusted in the gums.
Greatly of times there is on the gum flesh adjusted, which the ancients called albulus, therefore, you should suspend that with an onci or that you have that with the forceps and incision that you must take its root, and let the healing run or the blood.
And then place over the location incense blood or other styptic or excitative powders.
And if that flesh returns, then incision that secondarily, and cauterize that, because the cautery will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
Of the reason of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth of the parts of the teeth and of the mouth and between the gums are adjusted rough, jagged, and some times are of black and are made citrine or green until the gum sees by that corruption and are made the teeth jagged.
And therefore, you should make the patient sit between your hands and place the head of him in your knee, and scrape the teeth in which those roughnesses and causes resembling sand appear until none of them remain, and that do of the blackness and of the citrinity and of the others until they are modified.
Therefore, if this is what is at the beginning of the reason, it is good; and if not, then repeat the reason on those the other day and the second and the third until you come to the last statement in what you want.
And know that the teeth have need of various forms and figures of drills according to how they are prepared for your operation. And because the razors which the tooth is cut from the root are different from the razors which are used between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of razors you must prepare as you shall see forthwith:
Chapter .xxx.
Of the Extraction of Teeth.
Take care that you cure the tooth of its pain with all care and take your time in the entire extraction of it, for there is no cause that should supplant the whiteness of it when it is extracted, for it is substance sublime until the excuse in the extraction of it.
But take care when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, that you firmly ascertain which tooth is the painful one, for often the pain deceives the patient and makes it seem as if it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this by the operation of the barbers.
Therefore, as you must ascertain which tooth is the painful one, and that you know well, then take care that you scarify in the circuit of the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the tooth of the gums on all sides, and then make that with your fingers, or with subtle forceps at the beginning little by little until you reach the root.
And then with a strong grip hold it firmly with good communication or strong stupor, and place the head of the patient between your teeth that you hold well so that it does not hurt, and then pull the tooth according to rectitude in a manner that you do not break it, nor give the patient greater pain.
And therefore if it is not forgotten, then take one of those instruments, and put that behind it on all sides with facility and make an effort for the removal of it as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is broken or corroded, then take care that you compress with cloth that broken part and tighten it with the extremity of a fine ray so that it is not broken in the hour of your constriction upon it with your hold.
And take care that you operate exquisitely with scarification in the circuit of all parts of the gums and guard well your strength that you do not break that and remain a part of it, and return upon the patient by the malice that be greater than the first pain of it.
And guard yourself that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their fistinacio and audacity over the extraction of theirs, for they do not administer what we have recommended; for many times they make come upon the men illnesses great which are more difficult to be broken, and the roots of them remaugulated all, or some of those, or what you extract that with some bones of the mandible as many times as we have testified.
And after the extraction of it, wash the mouth with wine caut or wine with salt.
And if it is necessary for blood to flow thereby, as many times it is necessary, then take some quantity of zegi and sprinkle it over the wound, and if you do not confer that zegi, cauterize.
The form of the forceps and subtle hold with which the tooth is hurt at the beginning be of long extremity, of short handle and thick, so that they are not bent when with those you must operate.
And be as often of thick handle so that when you tighten with those they are not bent, be short extremities.
And be made of Indian iron, descentment made of straight extremities, and in the extremities of those be teeth of which some interlock in the others and tighten by strong constriction, and some times the extremities of those be the form of the beak of a bird which has the name ciconia, so it is called, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter .xxxi.

The extremities of those be made from the root as if it were a file or rasp.

And be of short extremity thick a little, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if by that the root is broken, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is of triangular extremity in which there is some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of a more subtle extremity.
And now you have helped with this instrument having two ramps of which this is the shape approaching the following: And with other instruments which precede in the shape of teeth; because the scraping is done with this instrument which is assembled like a large onion, and this is the shape:
Of the triangular, twisted or short extremity, which is some thickness, and not embedded so that it is not twisted.
And another knows that the instruments of the moving teeth are so and similarly the other instruments.
But the skillful and subtle artificer in the art, finds for himself according to what the operation and the diverse ailments mean to him; because there are ailments to which the ancients did not say nor put proper instruments, for the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is twisted, or if it may, then it should be cured over there in its place with what is convenient of the instruments and the forceps which we have said in the extraction of the roots of the teeth, and help with the gesture of which this is the shape:

But if in the bone there is a small puncture, then root that of that puncture and of its blackness, and cure that until it is healed.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of the prominent teeth above the others.
When the teeth are prominent not according to their natural course, for those the shape is this, and most when it befalls women, and most to the venerable ones; therefore, as you consider: if a tooth is nothing near another tooth, and it is not possible to suture or cement it, then root that; but that it is not annexed to another tooth, then you cut that with this instrument of which this is the shape; and it is similar to a pig's snout, and be made of Indian iron of a sharp, moving extremity.
And be your incision with which you cut that in too many days because of the hardness of the tooth, and that it is not damaged another of the teeth.
And if the prominent tooth is exposed to filing, then file that with a file of Indian iron of which this is the shape:
And be all of Indian iron the margin and well-made; and be of small, moving points as much as a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth be filed little by little in many days with facility, so that no other tooth be damaged and broken. And then line that at the end, and root that with some razors.
And if the tooth of which one part is twisted and it hurts the tongue, then as you file that until the sharpness of that fracture is stopped and leveled, and be filled so that the tongue does not hurt it and the word does not break.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver filings.
When accidents happen to the teeth in front with concussion and removal by percussion or by falling, and the patient cannot strengthen over that which he eats, and you have cured that with styptic medicines, and it has not been of any value, then because it does not break, it is made in those that are strengthened with gold filings and not with silver, because silver becomes green and soon it may be seen, but gold at no time is changed.
And be the filings in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that it can enter between the teeth; and the form of the straight one is that you take the filing and take double of it between two healthy teeth, and then that you grow with two tortuosities of the filing between two damaged ones, or if it is one or many, until the thread reaches the healthy tooth of the other side. And then iterate the testing into the part from which you began, and strengthen your hand with facility and wisdom so that it is not damaged.
And be your tightening with which you strengthen the filings must be the roots of the teeth so that it does not escape.
And then cut the superfluous extremities of the filings with the forceps or tweezers, and add those in each place with gesture, and convert them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that it does not cause injury to the tongue. And then leave it so tight as a Roman.
But if it is loosened or broken, strengthen that or those with another filing in such a manner in all its time.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the straight line of two healthy teeth and two damaged ones, as often these are tied:
And when it is done that a tooth or two is returned to its place after the fall of each one in its place, and they are regrown as we have recounted, and they are remade.
And that is done only by a good artificer and wise.
And sometimes it is fitted some cause of the bones of the cow, and it is made as the form of a tooth, and it is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is tightened as we have said, and remains and serves with that for a long time.
Chapter .xxxiv.
Of the incision of the ligament which comes in the tongue and prohibits speech.
This ligament which comes from the tongue some times or is natural with which man is born, or accidental and happens by plague which is already solidified.
And the operation in it is that men open the mouth of the patient, his head existing in the tieu se; and elevates his tongue, and then incides that ligament nerve according to the latitude between that the tongue is released from its recess.
But if in that there is some hardness or knot, and this is due to the consolidation of plague, then you put an ointment and split that by the latitude between that you have incised the ligament and that the knot is dissolved.
And guard yourself that no fissure is made in the flesh of the preon and that you split here artery and blood comes.
And then wash the vestiges of the incision with water or with wine vinegar or with hot water; and then place beneath the tongue a linen; so is the which holds the patient's tongue in each night so that it is not consolidated another time.
But if there happens a flux of blood, place on the location vitriol trissat; but if the blood comes out, then cauterize the location with a cauterizing iron which convenes to it. And then this cure with the other causes within that it is healed.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the extraction of a ranula from the tongue.
Sometimes it happens that on the tongue an abscess resembling a small lump appears, prohibiting the tongue from its natural operation; and often it is magnified while the mouth is opened.
And the operation in it is that men open the mouth of the patient, seeing before the sun and consider the abscess.
And if you see it of dark color or of black, hard, to which the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch that because it is gangrenous.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is humidity, then you put the ointment in it, and incise that with a subtle spatula, and incise it from all sides; and if blood comes to you in the hour of your operation, then you place above it trissat zegi, that is vitriol, so that it restrains the blood.
And then return to your work until you have treated it with compliment of it, until it is washed with wine vinegar and salt; and cure that with the other cure with water until it is healed.
Chapter .xxxvi.
Of the extraction of abscesses of the ears and of those that are in the throat and of the other abscesses.
When in the throat it is swollen, so are the glands which are assembled with a hard lump, which happen outwards and are called boils, which when you cure them with water as we have said in the division and it is not healed, then you consider if the abscess is hard, of dark color, of little sensation, then do not touch that with iron.
And if it is of red color and the root is thick, then do not touch that with iron because of the flux of blood; leave it alone until it matures, and then either you will extract it, or it will burst by itself.
And if it is of white color, soft, and the root of it is subtle, then that is the one that must be incised.
And the operation in it is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess of it has already rested it with a complete rest or if it is diminished with some diminution; and then make the patient stand before the sun, and his head be in the tieu se, and open his mouth, and the servant takes between his hands, pressing the tongue of it with a noble instrument of which this is the form:
And it is made of silver or of brass, and it is subtle like a knife.
Therefore, as you press with that the tongue and it is shown to you the abscess, and cast upon that your gaze, then take ointment and place it in the opening, and pull that to the outside parts as much as you can. Other water that you pull some cause of the tunics, and then incise that with the instrument of which this is the form:
Other water that you pull some cause of the tunics, and then incise that with the instrument of which this is the form: And it is assembled like forceps, but the extremities of it are curved, so that the orifice of each one is in the straight of the other, very sharp, the one which is of iron indi embedded.
But if this instrument is not at your disposal, then incise that with a spatula of which this is the form of it: And it is sharp on one part, not sharp on the other part.
And since you have incised a furrow, incise the other according to this same manner of incision, and then the incision the patient gargle with hot water.
But if there happens a flux of blood, gargle with water of decoction of the bark of millefolium or of myrtle leaf and similar to that of the stiptic causes so that the flux of blood is restrained, and then cure that until it is healed.
And sometimes there are born in the throat other abscesses other than those boils, and incise those according to that which I have said in the incision of two furrows equally.
And I have already treated a woman with an abscess which was in the throat of her, the abscess tended to a brown color, of little feeling, the one which had already closed the throat of her, and the woman breathed with tight meat, and had already prohibited to her eating and drinking of water, and she had already arrived at death if may be for a day or for two.
And the abscess had already grown so much that there were two fistfuls of pus inside which came out through two tracts.
Therefore, I treated it fistularly and put myself in one of the openings, and I pulled out that one, and threw it away from it a large portion, and then I incised that one which I understood from the other tract, and then I opened the mouth of it, and I compressed its tongue, and then I put the opening in that abscess.
And then I abscessed a large part of it, and it did not run from it except a little of blood. Therefore, the throat of that woman was absolutely cleared, and she began to drink water, and then she ate food, therefore I did not stop inciding that abscess many times for a long time, and abscesses succeeded in place of that one, the one which incision entered as it was prolonged.
Therefore I put a needle and cauterized the throat of her, therefore as much as an addition; and then I walked around and I did not know what God did of that one from me.
Chapter .xxxvii.
Of incision of a boil when it is abcessed.
When catarr arises on the boil, and it is abcessed and it is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the part below thick and round, and then it is called a bubo.
But when it is cured with the water that we have said in the division, and it does not compare your healing, and it seems sometimes that the abscess cauterized already is gone from it and it is subtle, then cover that you incised that which is aggregated round; and if it is to that longitude, and it is sanguine, of brown color or of black, and it is not to it feeling, then cover that you attempt the incision of it, because in that there is fear over the patient.
And when you see that according to the manner that I have said of its whiteness and length, that you make the patient lie down and press the tongue of him with an instrument of which you process its form and then put the opening in the navel, and pull that one down and incise that one with one of the two instruments the ones which are said in the incision of two melons.
And cover that you do not incise that one but the water that is adjusted over the natural term its addition, because if you incise of it more, you will lead his speaking.
And after the incision, use of water the one which have been recommended in the incision of two melons, and cure that one until it is healed.
But if the patient was frightened by the incision of it, cover that you use of a needle in the cauterization of it its fear, and the manner of the cautery in it is not but with sharp water, and it is that you put the head of the patient in your knee, and then press the tongue of him with the instrument the one which we have said, and then take of the water the one which we have said in the chapter of cauterization, and compare with that living cause, and put that not thick nor subtle, and of that fill the concavity of the instrument the one which this is the form:
Be the extramitat of it concavity in which is put the medicine as well as the concavity of a spoon of ray, and put the instrument with the medicine on the boil, and the patient yawns on his side so that it runs saliva or mucus of the mouth of that one through the intrinsic medicine, so that it does not descend from that one for any cause over the throat of him and that it cleanses that one.
And with your hand to the medicine, and express that one on the uvula for the space of half an hour until you see that one being darkened and sedated by the bite of the medicine.
And if you want, take a cloth and envelopes that one over the extremity of the ray, and submerge the cloth in the medicine, and introduce the ray with the cloth in a tube of over until the cloth adheres in that tube, and do this many times until you can achieve that which you want of the cauterization of the uvula, and then remove that one because it dries and falls down after three days or four.
And if you needed that you repeat the medicine, repeat that one, and after the cauterization abstergeys in the circuit of the uvula with cloth dipped in border, and modify with that water that is in the circuit of it of the medicine, and be washed with hot water, and be cured for the moment with embrocation, and dedicate with gargles until it is healed.
And this kind of cure is more saving than incision and more long of fear.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with water that is more subtle than cauterization and incision, because it is cured with fumigation in this manner: receive calamine, hyssop, oregano, rue, sicamous arabic, chamomile, broth and similar to those of the herbs, and adjust all those causes, or some of them, in a pot, and let them be submerged in hot water, and let the pot be well cleaned decently, and let there be in the middle of the pot a hole, on which is placed an instrument concavity of silver according to this manner; it is made of silver or of silver.
And the extremity is introduced into the mouth of the patient until the vapor reaches the uvula over the tongue, until the uvula is completely obscured.
And then repeat over those moist areas until it is dry, and be careful not to do this cure at the beginning of the development of abscesses, because it would too much tighten the abscess, and instead of doing this, you must declare the abscess of it with a cautery.
But if you do not have this instrument present, then take a cane and on the extremity of it make a hollow of wax, so that the mouth of the patient be covered, because the wax prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this cure is good for health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Of the extraction of splinters and of what adheres in the throat and of other causes.
Moist patches adhere to the throat or a splinter of wood or any other cause.
Therefore, it is necessary that you extract what appears of those things, on which the gaze falls, because you press the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that it is declared to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear and is to you hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because often the cause annexed to the throat is forcibly ejected by vomiting; or that the patient swallows a piece of bread or a root of lettuce or a piece of dry bread; or you press a piece of dry sea sponge, and tie that firmly with a thread, and then the patient swallows that, because as it arrives at the place where the spines are and are extracted, then they are forcibly ejected. And moreover, take an eel and tie it firmly with a thread, and it is placed and gently extracted; or take a porr and gently the tip and gently the other end.
But if they are not forcibly ejected by those causes which we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form:
