On the section for the ailment of nafir.
This ailment is called nafir in our land, and it is pain that comes in certain limbs, and then it is changed from limb to limb.
And I have already seen this as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein of the arm, and as it was for an hour, I saw that inflammation go as if a worm was pushing it, but more gently than being heavy, as if it were living silver when it runs from place to place.
And then it went into the pain of that place and ended in the muscle, and then it stayed there for an hour.
And then it corrected in the other body until it reached the other arm.
And then I recommended to myself that I should turn it all over her body as I saw; and it was too marvelous of the permutation of it and the speed from limb to limb. And I had already seen this before in my own feet in that manner, however, I have seen many that suffer pain being changed from limb to limb. But I did not see this in the feet as in the woman.
And I did not think it to be anything but that because the woman was old, or so of dry body and of exposed veins, and in this appearance, that windiness was perceived.
And it is necessary that it does not appear according to this retro-healing in those who are delicate and with humid bodies of well-nourished veins.
And as you want the cure of it, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not to you apparent in the temple as we have said, then you tighten on that and leave it lightly and press on that until that windiness is constricted, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it in your own temples, then you cure it with the shaking of the body and with what you expel windiness, and charms those with fetid pills and with pills of serapi and similar things.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to the species, and according to the places of the body in which they strike, for their diversity is that of those that have large tips, and small, and concave, and blunt, they are solid; and of those that have three angles, and of those that have tongues; and of those that have shafts.
And of the signs of the service is when it strikes in it, and it pierces the bone, and it is brought out of the cypharynx which is above the brain, for it happens through it soda vehement, and scotomia, and vertigo, and roaring in the feet, and inflammation and roaring of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of understanding and vomiting of bile, and very strong is the speech cut off and the voice is destroyed, and it is brought out of the place of the wound a white humidity resembling powder, and eyes of it resembling the fluidity of the flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then you abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, if it is not outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it strikes the heart, are that it is near the left nipple, and it is felt with that which is put in a hard cause, and it is not a cause void; and very strong the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the hand, and the running of the wound black blood, and it feels that coldness of the extremities, and a great cold sweat, and syncope; and therefore know that death descends upon it all at once.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are so easily of spumy blood from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are inflamed, and the color of the patient is changed, and he expires with great spirit, and demands the attraction of cold air.
And if it strikes the arrow in the ligament which is in the feet, and the pain is then near the small of the back, and it is made the leg great with vehement pain and with sighing, and it is not good all the members of the muscles.
And if it strikes the arrow in the stomach, then very strong eyes of the wound food any undigested.
And if it strikes the arrow in the belly, and it is put in it, eyes some wound of the wound, or the cloth, or the intestine which is dislocated, and there is no hope in the cure of it extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow has entered the bladder, and urine has been expelled, and something has been processed from it to the outside parts, and a great deal of pain is on the patient, then you know that it is mortal.
May the other members as well as the face, neck, throat, and arms, and legs and their counterparts, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the ribs, and the legs and their counterparts, be saved according to the major dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you that I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may signify this for your treatment.
The cause is, that in a man a  arrow was shot into the lacrimal of his nose at the root, and you extracted that from the other side, leaving the gray of the ear of him, and he is healed, and it did not cause harm to his nose.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew who was shot in his eye, leaving the eyelid as it was, and the arrow was already hidden and could not be obtained from it except for a small extremity with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion's head on it in which there were no ears.
And the Jew healed, and it did not cause any harm to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which was shot in the throat, and it was an Arab arrow, and it is the one that has ears; and therefore I incised over it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and therefore I subtly approached it, and then extracted it, and the Christian healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who was shot in his belly, and I thought he would die from it; and therefore, because forty days had passed and nothing had changed in his condition, I incised over the arrow and maneuvered it, and he healed, and it did not cause him any harm.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow was shot in his back, and the wound was consolidated on him, and because it was almost seven years, he pulled the arrow out at the root of the hip.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow was shot in her belly, and the wound was consolidated within her, and it was not changed in her condition for a long time, and the woman did not suffer any harm in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow was shot in his face and the wound was consolidated, and he broke the arrow, not feeling much pain, and he looked quite well.
And I extracted the arrow from the man of the king's princes who was shot in the middle of his nose and had already declined to the right side a little, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his treatment shortly after the arrow was shot, within three days.
And then I examined the wound of the arrow very carefully; and then I inquired about it with a subtle touch, and I did not feel it; but I felt a prick below the right ear, and then I suspected that that prick was because of the extremity of the arrow.
And I plastered the place with a plaster which has attractive and digestive virtue, confessing to myself that the place was festering so that it appeared to me the place of the arrow, so that it would dry over it.
And it did not cause any harm in the place, significant that the arrow had reached that place; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and it did not cause any harm; and the wound was consolidated meanwhile, and he broke the wound from the extraction of it for a space of many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and recounted this to me.
And then I placed on the wound a medicine acute and corrosive for many days until it was open, and with the touch I felt it, and examined the extremity of the arrow subtly, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I adjusted it in the appearance of the wound with that acute medicine until it appeared to me visibly the extremity of the arrow, and it took me time with that for a space of time within four months.
And then because the wound was amplified, and it was possible for me to intervene with forceps in it, and I removed it, and it did not cause any harm, then I did not cease to subtly and ingeniously work on it with manners of engineering, until one day I began on it with manners of engineering to hang it with forceps skillfully made, according to how they will come in the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored that wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose was not restored, and I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the patient healed completely, and he did not suffer in it in any way.

Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins of phlebotomy of which it is customary to phlebotomize .xxx., of which are in the head .xvi., There are two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called alhasisain; two herbs that are in the two temples appear; and two veins that are in two lacrimals of two bones that are called aspicientz; and the prepared vein in the middle place of the forehead, and the vein that is in the extremity of the nose; and two guidegi that are in the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins that are called algelic —; and there are two veins that are below the tongue. Because that man who has tender flesh of rare pores, you must scarify him once and no more, because the place should not be ulcerated.
And command the ventor to amplify the scarification and prolong it a little, and temper the suction with facility.
And if in the blood there is thickness, then you must scarify twice, because in the first scarification the way of subtlety to the blood and its liquidity is opened. But in the second, to the acquired scrutiny of the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then the third scarification should be made so that it may achieve the last.
And finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a little, one scarification suffices us.
And if we want an extraction of much blood, we scarify with many scarifications; and if we knew that the blood was thick, then we must scarify with a prolonged scarification.
And the term of scarification in which is the depth of the incision only.
And if that one whose incision is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, then the places of the incisions should be anointed more gently, wider, more resolved; if it is in summer, as it is with oil of aloe or oil of violet, or oil of double amaranth, or oil of quince; and if it is in winter, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or of sanc, and to those similar.
And if the superfluity is thick, hot, then the oil should be amaranth, or oil of balsam, or oil of aloe, or oil of anise, and to those similar.
But if the ventor has wide pores of tender flesh, then he should be prohibited from oil, and he should have the places of the incisions near their own ventilation washed with water or with cold water of solatrum, or with quince water, or with water of board, and similar to those.
And the one whose blood is of much humidity, wash their incisions with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar to those.
And of the one whose superfluities are thick, wash their incisions with old wine, or with water of sansuc, which is majoram, or of decoction of anise, or of chamomile, and to those similar.
And the incision should be guarded before bathing; bathing should be administered near the bath for an hour or two, and he should sleep near the incision.
What is what is necessary to be regulated by the ventor before phlebotomy.
You must consider at the beginning: then if the ventor or the one who is to be phlebotomized is choleric and tends to sharp blood and inflammation, then he should be prepped with cooling causes, such as vinegar, endive with sour wine and lettuce, and secanabin, and julep, and to those similar.
And pause their meals as much as they can, and birds' meat, and dishes seasoned with sour things, and to those similar.
And of the one whose complexion is cold, he should be prepped to drink syrup of almiva, or syrup of alsecanabin of seeds, and sumac, and very fragrant wine between veal and lamb; and that he should be commanded to use little drinking and eating, and pause their meals of little and canary, and sparrows and small birds and doves.
And he should drink more than eat on the day of incision and phlebotomy.
And he should be given to drink in some hours to certain men tyriaca alpharac, or medicines of musk, so it is muscat, or alcelita before the incision or before phlebotomy or near it, so that the main limbs be comforted and that the blood be subtiliated; and he should not take that scalfatz.
But the wounds that are the incisions should be placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the places of the joints, and on the tip of the hip, because these members do not endure incisions on themselves.
And it is not desired for those (wounds) but rather the drawing of blood from the limb, just as we place a cupping glass on the nipple in the disease of bleeding from the nose; and we use it so that the cold, viscous air from the limb is dissolved, just as we place it on the belly and navel; because it thins the limb and warms it and removes the pain as it resolves the cupping glass.
And it is already placed on the abscesses when they occur in their suppuration or boil, because by virtue of the strong drawing, the pus is drawn out and eradicates the boil from its place.
And similarly it is done when it is placed on the liver and spleen, drawing out the cupping glass in those enclosed spaces.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with suction alone; and some times they are administered with fire.
And some times they are administered full of tepid water in the diseases of the spleen.
And this is because the cupping glass should be filled and be larger, so it is either water alone or water in which herbs have been boiled appropriate to the water.
Form of cupping glass administered with fire: its orifice width is two fingers, as we have formed it; and the quantity of the preon is the majority of a palm; and it is in the place of it, in the place that is in the circuit, the majority of it, the trauc is small according to the quantity that enters it a needle.
It is made of a thick piece of iron or copper, with large edges, lion, smooth and rounded so that it does not damage the limb of its position on it.
And in the middle of it is a transverse bar of iron or steel, on which a candle is placed with the fire.
And already this cupping glass is larger than the one we have described; and the small one less according to the diseases and the state of him who uses it.
And the cupping glasses of the infants and the weakened are others than the cupping glasses of the young and of large bodies.
And the quality of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is that it extends the fire with wood decently in front of a small candle of wax, and places it on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle place of the cupping glass, so that the fire is ignited around the part below so that it does not burn the body of the patient.
And then it is placed on the limb of the patient, and the finger is on the trauc that we have said, and when we have the cupping glasses and do not indicate that we show the finger and that it escapes the vapor on that trauc, then the cupping glass is immediately dissolved.
And properly ignite the fire according to its manner, and repeat that if they are indicated of it.
But in a cupping glass that is administered in the disease of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar nor trauc, and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and it is placed on the limb alone.
And this is the form of it: And this cupping glass, the more it is large, the better, because it can receive a large quantity of water.

Bloodletting is not administered in all members but in those members in which it is not possible to place cupping glasses, because of the smallness of those, such as the mouth and gums and similar to them; or in a member denuded of flesh, such as the finger and the nose and their similar.
And the quality of using those that have been found leeches that are in the waters of muddy ponds.
And then they are left in the day and night in muddy water until they are famished, and that no cause remains in the belly of those; and then the body is evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and with cupping glasses; and then the limb of the patient is rubbed until it reddens; and then they are placed on it.

Thus begins the reading of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations that occur in the bones.
Oh, son, know that these foolish doctors and the vulgar boast of this chapter, and those who at no time have regarded the books of the Ancients nor read even a letter from them.

And therefore I turn to experiment and study in the length of my life.

First Chapter.
Summaries and collections from the essence of bone fractures, from which their preliminary knowledge is necessary before we begin the recollection of broken and diverse members, of each one. Therefore, I say that in the prologue of this chapter, the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters to the understanding of which at the beginning the artificers are forced and stand on the truth of them; so it is for you and those who are prompt to know or learn this noble art other than you.
And therefore I say that when you come across a fracture or dislocation or sprain or wound, then you must at the beginning apply yourself to phlebotomy or to bleed that, or both at once, if no cause prohibits it, as for example, weakness of virtue, or the one to whom you are attending is a child, or old and decrepit, or the time is of vehement heat, or of vehement cold.
And then he should be content in his food with cold herbs and poultry and crabs; and we forbid him fatty meats and filling him with foods and all foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are sure of a safe abscess, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his first regimen to which he is accustomed.
And therefore, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed with nourishing foods of much and heavy nourishment in which there is viscosity, such as foods made of cooked wheat with water, and rice, and caps, and fish, and cow bellies, and eggs, and fresh poultry, and thick gruel and similar to those, and with this regimen is made the coagulation of the fracture more lightly and better.
And know that the broken bones when they are in men who are in the first youth and in the decrepit, it is not possible that they be continued and consolidated according to their nature by the drying of their limbs.
However, nature adjusts and amends over the broken bone of every part any cause seeming to glut in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens it until they are at once conglutinated and tied together, until they come to the end of firmness and hardness, as fire at the beginning, until no cause prohibits it from its works.
And for this reason it is necessary that you pause the patient's foods, foods in which there is heaviness and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the hand; and similarly all the other members are diverse among themselves.
And you will come with recollection of every species of fracture, explained in their distinct chapter from the other.
Many varieties of fracture within themselves. And they are diversified the species of fracture of the bone in themselves; for there is an arched fracture, another that happens in stars; and sometimes it is made a fracture according to the length of the bone: and in the fractures of their satagia and stars and additions proceeding and non-proceeding, as it is done in the pen when it is prepared to write, and it is a fracture with a plaque and disruption in the head, and it is a small fissure fracture.
And to every species is engendered a proper torment in its restoration, according to which will come recollection of it distinct in its places.
Many signs of fracture of the bone. And by this is known a bone fracture: it is the twisting of it, and of that prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and so of it when you compress it with your hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor is there a feeling when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not feel great pain, then it is not a fracture here.
However, it is possible that it is a small twist or fissure, so that you must feel it with your hand and with compression of the whole; however, place on it of the medicines of which you remember a little before this moment, the one that is convenient to the place; and then be strict by subtle constriction.
And know that the place when it is arched and is not split into two or otherwise that does not happen in it, as if the whole part of its comparison is remauged, then you must begin from your hour to the rectification of it, however, that it happens after the cautery abscess.
And if it happens abscess caut, then leave that for a few days until the abscess ceases; and then bandage it according to whatever manner that you can of lightness and of ingenuity.
And know that the restoration of it and the healing of it is lighter than of the bone in which this happened sathagie.
And then strengthen it according to what will come the remembrance of it.
But if in the bone there are satagias, then it is necessary the extension of this broken limb of two parts, if it is the arm or the leg; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords or with one cord and the hand.
And be in the position with which you place the limb in the place, according to its natural figure, while the parts are extended of the broken limb, then you must perforate that you make return those additions with ingenuity and according to whatever manner that you can in their places.
And guard yourself according to your strength that it does not happen upon the patient by your operation pain nor injury; and perforate yourself according to your power that you apply one bone with the other its companion according to their best forms.
And you must in that hour profitably touch with your hand that, and if you see here any other cause, rectify that and bandage that according to the quantity of your virtue.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as if foolish doctors do that, who often make happen abscess caut by their operation, or make happen contraction in the limb as we have seen this by the operation of their foolish practices.
And the quality of the constriction of the tranquil limb is that which I recommend; for that you know that the broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their greatness and in the form of those.
And of those that are small as the arm and the hand and the foot and similar to those, then you must the envelopment be of very fine cloth.
But those that are gross as the thigh or the leg, then you must in those be envelopment broad and hard, for the broad liament connects the great limb and strengthens that of all parts with even constriction, so that in it no macula enters.
And then, as you begin, your evacuation of equation, pause liament convenient to this, over the place of that fracture with three envelopment or four according to that the limb merits; and strengthen your hand a little in the liament.
And then, see to that at the part most sovereign of the place of the fracture, and strengthen less than not to force your constriction with which you have strengthened the place of the fracture. And then, lengthen yourself to envelopment of the place of the fracture a little, and soften the constriction little by little, until you take from the place a great part.
And then, take another bandage and envelop that other time over the broken place with envelopment, and then see to the envelopment at the part farther from the fracture; and be your operation in the constriction of the envelopment and in the softening of that according to that we have said in the first envelopment below.
And then, pause between the envelopment of stopa lion or cloth with which is evened the tortuosity of the fracture, if in it there is tortuosity; and if not, do not pause in it any cause.
And then, pause upon it another bandage, and then bandage over these envelopment stars decently made at once, if in the place there is no inflammation or abscess caut, and then pause upon it that which seats the abscess that and that os the inflammation; and leave that for a few days; and then you strengthen upon it the stars.
And let the stars be mostly made of cane, well formed; or of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each star be according to this form and this figure:
However, the star placed over the fracture should be thicker and a little larger than the others, and the length of the star according to what is measured on the limb in magnitude and smallness; and then bind over the stars with another bandage according to your first structure; and then tie it below decently according to what we have said of structure, and it is that it should be your structure according to the location of the fracture more, and as much as the fracture is elongated, the less the structure.
And that the threads be medium in thickness and fineness, and be of silk; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen by the operation of some fools putting their threads of cords of hemp, and it is a great error, when the bandage is outside equality.

And if the patient is said to be sensitive in the extremities of the stars near the bandage in those places, then place some cotton wool or carded wool between that so that it does not harm him.
Sometimes a fracture is with a plague, and accidents happen in the bone, and I will come with a remedy in that in the chapter according to singularity.
And know that all broken limbs are not straight with stars on the first day; the reason is that the limb, when it is great, should not be placed on stars but rather after 5 or 7 or more days, according to your certainty of the advent of a boil.

Chapter Second: Of fracture when it occurs in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are made by a blow of a spade, and the blow throws all the bones into the skull, which is left the bone, as if it were done in the shaft, and for this reason this species is called ascial; or the spade cuts into some part of the bone, and cuts into the flesh of it and does not pass the incision into the back of it; and this species of fracture is called absolute levitation.
And the plague of these two fractures is either great or small, and of a fracture that is contortion or attrition; and the cause of it is a blow with a pole or a fall on a rock, and their likenesses.
And this fracture is either penetrating that already approaches the panniculus which is left the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures is either great or small.
And of a fracture that is broken in the bone, in the subtlety of the small hair, and for this reason this species is called capillary fracture.
And it is a fracture that is made either by a fall or by a blow of a rock and its likenesses, which makes some lamina of the bone enter into the parts within, and it is made in the concave place as it happens to the payroles of eram when it happens to their blow and is the part of their entrance into the parts within.
And most often this is done in the bodies of wet bones, as if they were the heads of infants.
And to all these species of fractures, there are pains as if they were stars separated. And I will come with remembrance of all this in its place.
And we shall know all these species of fracture by detection upon them and by inquiry of them with touch and examination of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And the capillary species is known by this that it is discovered upon the bone, and it is rubbed, and lined upon it with touch, for the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of a fracture, consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and then if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification upon pain, as it is vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and abscess of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the joints and roar of them, and likenesses to them of the accidents in the major dispositions, then adopt that it is not healed.
And if you see no-flowing accidents, then you begin the cure of him.
The cause of this is that if the patient has come to you at the beginning as he is afflicted and this is in the days of winter, then you must study the condition of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you must examine the bone before the seventh day, because that which is left of the bone should be corrupted by the panniculus, and that the accidents that we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and it is in small pieces and attrition, then you must insert the minute and rubbed piece, according to what I will tell you.
And you shave the head of the afflicted patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as much as possible according to the figure of the plague, and that it be light upon the patient.
And if it happens to you to detect the bone, flows of blood or pus, then you oppose it with what you must, and it is that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and rubbed oil, and make the patient rest until the pus is settled, and if you are sure of the blood flow; and then you begin in the circulation of the bone and in its ablation.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.
And the one way is that you insert the bone with a straight, narrow incisor, and this is the shape of it: And then administer another incisor, this one a little wider than that one; and this is the shape of it:
And then administer more the wider incisor than the second.
And you must have various types of incisors, of which some are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others, and they are at the end of sharpness of their extremities, and they are made of Indian iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisor so that it does not break the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you must pierce in its circuit before you administer the incisors with the drills, which are called non-deep drills.
And they are not called thus, but because those do not reach the term of the bone of the skull, to which it is close, because the drill has a round extremity or margin, over which the remaining head is sharp, resembling a margin or a small circle, which prohibits it from being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a great number of which as many as are convenient to the thickness of each bone, so that it is to you present in all the tests of the skull, the extremity of which is sharp, the quantity in length and shortness be according to the quantity of thickness of this test.
And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of piercing in the circuit of the broken bone is that you place the drill on the bone and revolve it with your fingers until you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the drill to another location, and place that which is between all the cracks the quantity of the thickness of the ray or environment of that.
And then insert with incisors that which is between each crack of the bone, and make that as subtle as you can in the end of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause for some instruments which you will have prepared for that, as such as tweezers and subtle forceps.
And you must guard with all caution that the incisor does not touch any cause of the scalp.
And therefore, as you will have circuited the bone, and if it is continued with it, you will separate it from the scalp, and you will have extracted it, you must shave and smooth that in the roughness of it of that which is in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor, but however, it must be more thin and more subtle than the others incisors.
And if any small pieces of bones and splinters remain, then take that with ease with what you must of the instruments; and then care for the wound with lichens and ointments which we have said.
And the other way of operation is lighter, farther from pain, which Gualia and lauzec call by great praise; and this is the sermon of it: it is necessary to you at the beginning that you begin to discover the part of the bone of the place in which is the fracture most vehement and most manifest, until when you will have discovered that part and you will have placed below it the extremity of this slow and gentle incisor, of which this is the form:
And it is that the lenticular part is long and you do not insert any cause, and the acute part is of it in two sides forward in length, as sometimes; so that the lenticular part be adjusted to the scalp with the face of it incident acute in the bone.
And then strike upon the incisor of one part with a small mallet until you insert the whole bone with ease as it is revolved, and you are sure of the panniculus that no accident happens to it in any way, and if the medicant14 was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he was asleep.
If now any cause is annexed to the bone of the panniculus in some places of the bone, cut that of it with the extremity of the lenticular incisor, and deliver that of it with facility, because it is delivered from it its pain and its fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the panniculus, and what is cut off or taken from the bone is its fascia, and remains in it roughness and subtle ridges, then when you remove that roughness and eradicate those ridges with subtle razors, of which you have prepared the number, of diverse quantities so that it is possible for you to administer in each place the most convenient ones of them and the most right ones according to what you are applying in your operation that broken bone and its figure.
And when you administer in your reason with which razor you remove the bone at the beginning the most blunt of those razors, and then use the most subtle one of it, and do not cease doing so according to continuity until you arrive at administering the most thin and the most subtle of all of them.
But in each of the other fractures, requested capillary and subtle fractures, when you administer care according to what you perceive for its rectification.
And it is a cause that it is not well-known about all of those who have studied in that art. And this is what we have shown about those who have written about great fractures.
And if the panniculus is cut when you eradicate the bone, then when you take cloth according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge that in rose oil, and place that over the orifice of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place that over the first cloth, and do this as lightly as you can, so that you do not crush the cephalic; and then administer over that bandage, and do not tighten it except for the quantity that the wound alone has.
And leave that with this care for a day or two until you are sure of the abscess caut; and then untie that and then administer medicines with which is superfluous dryness, as well as root of linen and flour of herbs, and powder of incense and of aristolochia, and of similar things to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder that be pulverized over the wound, as well as they are dry; and universally, administer in that all medicine of property of which it is that it abstersca and does not bite.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and not be in it dirt of rinsing or other; nor allow virulence to be added to it; because when it is adjusted over the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts it and you can corrupt it, and it happens to the patient as if it were a bad and malignant evil.
And it happens in the cephalic of the head, as if from it is modified the bone and most when the cure of it is less cared for, and it is blackness on the surface of it; and therefore consider when it happens to the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is pernicious all of it.
And if it does not happen blackness but from the medicine that is placed on top of it, and it is in virtue of that medicine that it makes that blackness, then when you take of honey one part and of rose oil parts iii., and in those lines a cloth, and then place that over the cephalic; and then cure that with the species of cure that convenes until it is healed.
Chapter III.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken but two parts together, or one of those sometimes, because in it you have two bones, because the part left is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and does not happen to it except attrition and tortuosity and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then when you introduce the said little one into its nose, and that you align that fracture from within with your said finger, and with the said finger outside, until the nose returns according to its natural figure. And do this by you with facility, and guard yourself that no pain be caused to the patient by that operation of yours.
And if the fracture is in the most sovereign part of the nose, and the said one does not reach that, then when it be equal with the extremity of the ray in which it is small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the semblance of that.
And take care of you in the restoration of it in the first day of the fracture, if it is possible; and if not, then about the seventh or tenth, the rest of the abscess caut.
And then introduce into the extremity of the nose a piece of cloth of it, if the fracture is in one part, or put two splints, if the fracture is in two parts, and be in the splint thickness of such quantity that it fills the trough of the nose.
And I say some of the ancient restorers that the splints be enclosed in border, and be changed every day, but it is not seen to me; it should be that the piece be enclosed in album of comfit with powder of mol; and then leave the one until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes there are splints in the nose, in place of a splint, a kind of poultice wrapped in a lion's cloth; and if the patient already has a fracture of the nose, very violent and that the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do this, and if you want, do splints.
And if another time a boil occurs after the cautery, plaster the nose with cerot or with cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if no boil occurs after the cautery, then cover that area with a plaster of sesame flour and powdered quince seeds with egg white; and place a lion's cloth over it, and do not touch the nose with any cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then cover those with a splint, and extract those with instruments that are suitable for those; and then close the incision, and cure it with what will heal and consolidate it, and with plasters suitable for it.
And if a plague occurs in the nose, then cover it with splints and administer lead pipes until it is completely healed.
Chapter .iiii.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture of it is not with a plague, regard then if the fracture is only outward, and not broken in two and curved at the parts within, then its knowledge is lighter.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you introduce your left index into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left mandible, then introduce the index finger of your right hand; and push with that the flexibility of the fracture within with ease to the outward parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; with that decentment equal that.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated in two, then cover that you administer extension of two parts according to rectitude until it is possible the equation of it.
And if there occurs concussion of teeth to disruption, then strengthen those of which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or lead; and place on the broken jaw cerot, and place on it doubled cloth, and place on the cloth a large splint made decentment, or a piece of sole equal to the length of the jaw; and tie that above according to how the ligation of it is prepared for you and that the application convenes, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use of rest and tranquility, and place the food of him soft leons.
And if you estimate that already the figure is altered in any manner, then you take off and untie that on the third day; and then rectify that which is already altered of it and plaster it with powdered moli, with egg white, or with sesame flour, because you will have obtained the cerot of it, and place on the plaster lion's wool.
And therefore, as it stands annexed and adjusted that plaster on it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, that you do not untie it until it is healed and strengthened the fracture; because often times this fracture is straightened in three sempmanas.
And if another time a boil occurs after the cautery, then administer that which we have said many times in the sedation of it until it is healed, and be removed that boil.
And if the fracture is with a plague, then consider if it is a piece of the bone separated or splints, then you take subtilia those splints with what you convenes to it in the removal of those from the instruments.
And if the orifice of the plague is strait, then you amplify it with the fleubotomi according to the quantity of your necessity.
And after you will have obtained those splints, and of those no sir remaugut re, then you cover the orifice of the plague if it is wide; and if not, place on that one of the plasters that conveneysso to it and incarnate that, until it is healed.
Chapter .v.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken at the part before the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three manners.
Because it is broken, or separated, or is separated in two parts other that do not coincide splints; and it is easy to the restoration of it; or it is so that it occurs in it splints, and it is difficult to the restoration of it; or it is so that the fracture is with a plague.
And the operation in that, when the fracture is with a plague, is that you make to be present two ministers, so that one has the adjutori and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck with that part.
And after enguala the fracture with your fingers until it is made the form of it according to what cove, and not be in it little eminence nor concavity.
And if you indigeysses more of extension, then cover below the titillic of the patient's hopes of cloth or wool, and be the greatness of it according to the quantity of your necessity; and extend and elevate the clavicle and constrict the hope with your hand until you enguales the frectura according to what cove.
And if you cannot pull the extremity of the clavicle outwards, because it has reached the breastbone, then make the patient lie on their neck, and place a medium-sized cushion on their muscle.
And the minister presses the muscle towards the lower parts while the bone of the clavicle is elevated that is in the breastbone, and then you rectify the fracture and grasp it with your hands.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are bad, then make them over that and extract those splinters with ease.
And if it is held in the bone, then you make an incision of it with one of the scissors which you have prepared for this, because you had placed below the clavicle the instrument that serves the chisel; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is the form of it:
And it is similar to a chisel of which this is the concavity; and its width according to what you have need of it, according to the greatness of the bone or the smallness of it.
But the length of it be according to what is possible to you in the operation; that it have two extremities thus as times, and one extremity be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you have extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and it is sure of a cautery abscess, then you adjust the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a cautery abscess, then you  apply the wound with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the scissure of the wound.
And if an abscess occurs, then you put a cloth in strong wine and rose oil and wine, and place it over that.
And all, that it not be bad the board over the broken bone; and for so much as you see the patient every day, that when the ligament is softened or sometimes the board already be remauguda, then you rectify that and strengthen the bandage.
And then place the sleep of the patient on their neck, and place below the tip of it of their sleep at night a small cushion; so that with that it be elevated the support of it from the side of it, and therefore is elevated the fracture of the clavicle to elevation of the muscle; or it be the arm of it tied to the neck of it.
And do not untie the bandage unless you happen in the place proximately or abscess, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it to that, and adjust in the stricture and restore that and leave it until it be restored and be coagulated the fracture of the clavicle.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in twenty-eight days, and some times in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter VI.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula tart is broken in the side of it and not broken of it but the edges.
And therefore when it is broken the place of it — and it is broken in the middle place of it — and it is not known except by touch, then according to what is the figure of the fracture forces you the preparation of it and reduce that according to the natural figure in every manner of ingenuity which is to you possible.
And then place over the place powder of wheat with egg white and linen cloth; and then place powder of cloth doubled; and then place over it a board slice of fine board according to the quantity of the spatula whole or maybe a little.
But if below the board is concavity in some of the places of the spatula, then you embrace that concavity with linen cloth, and then raise the board according to equality.
And then strengthen above with humidity long with constriction decent and with firmness of the board until it not be remauguda from its place.
And consider the bandage every day; and every time that it be softened, tie another time and strengthen it and equal the board if it be remauguda from its place.
And place the patient on their side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And therefore when this number is complete, then you dissolve the bandage, and that you know for sure that it is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the location of the lower bone and place the bandage below the hip, then you cut across it, and remove it, and do as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle of the saddle, if an abscess occurs here, cauterize it.
Chapter VII.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle part of it and that is hard to mend; but the extremities of it must be mended that so they may be cicatrized and comminuated.
And of the accidents of it when the middle part of it is broken, it is that it declines to the parts of it and occurs great pain to it, and difficulty in its motion, and cough, and great heat and vomiting of blood; and it is declared in it the cavity in the broken bone and is not concealed to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that it be laid the patient on his back, and place between the spaces of it a cushion; and then press on the muscles of it, and adjust the bones of it with the hands of two sides, and subtilly in the operation of it and in the equation according to every manner that is to this possible and that convenes to this, until that the figure of the bone returns according to that cove.

Chapter VIII.
Of the restoration of the back when it is broken.
Know that fracture of the back does not catch but in places gross that follow the back; and of the extremities of them of the part in front does not occur to those contricio but for that which so cartilaginous. And the restoration be thus that you equal the fracture with the said according to the manner possible, until that it be engulfed according to that cove, and then plaster and strengthen the broken bone with a stela, if it be indigested of that.
And if the fracture of the back is declining to the parts of it, then occurs to the patient great pain and puncture, as well as puncture that occurs to him who has pleureci, and for that which the bone puts the velament; and occurs to it difficulty of handling, and cough, and vomit of blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the Antixs are ingenious in that with much ingenuity.
And of them is that which is said: he who places foods of the patient that engendered inflation and flatulence, for that the belly be inflated and be extended, and the fracture be impelled to the parts outside.
But we would not avow that for that which it not be cause lighter to generate abscess caut, if it does not occur; and if it already occurs, then it already helps to that.
And they would say some of them that it be laid upon a ventouse and then that it is sucked with force; and it is seeming to ratiocination, but however it is feared that the ventouse drives the superfluities to the place by the weakness.
And they would say some of them: he who the place be covered with wool that already is submerged in hot oil, and that pulvils be placed between the ribs until that it be filled, that it be the bandage equal when it envelops that according to roundness; and then be cured the patient with cure of pleurisy of food and of medicine.

And then that they adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is great, with suturing, and cure that with plasters until that it be healed.

And be laid upon the side upon which he sleeps, until that it be healed.
Chapter IX.
Of the restoration of fracture of the vertebra of the back and of the neck.
Sometimes it must fracture to the bones of the neck, and it is hard to mend them, and seemingly to the vertebra of the back. And as that occurs to some and you want to know if it will heal or no, then consider, for if times their hands already softened and stupefied and dead and not can more those nor extend nor strengthen those and when you constrain those with your said or prick those with a needle and no feel in those pain, then you know that it is not healed, and in the most of dispositions it is mortal.
And if he feels those constrictions with his fingers and punctures, then you know that the bone's neck is saved and that the patient will recover with care.
And if you see in the spine of the neck the appearance of that and want to know if it is healed or not, then consider the feet of him.
Then if you see that they are already softened, accustom yourself to that which happens in the limbs, and when you press on their back, feel the wind and the urge, and sometimes he is turned on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and therefore do not work in his cure.
But if you do not see any cause of those things, the cause is better and less serious.

Chapter X.
Of the Restoration of the Hip.
The bones of the hips are often broken, and if they break, the fracture of them is nothing but a communication in the extremities of those and are incised in the length and decline to the parts of the body; and it happens to the patient pain in the place and puncture, and the leg is stunned when it is a fracture.

And if the fracture is in the extremity of it only, then you align that according to what it is fitted by equation aligning until it is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long or which already declines to the parts below, then you make the patient lie on his belly until it is prepared to you the restoration of that fracture. Chapter XI.
Of the Restoration of the Fracture of the Shoulder.
The shoulder is that which is between the armpit to the head of the shoulder.

And be tied at two extremities two bandages, and then be suspended from the elevated place.

And then suspend over that some heavy cause, or the minister extends that to the parts below; and then the physician and that the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns as you cover.
And the other way is that the patient be turned on his neck and suspend the arm of his neck with a bandage; and then command two ministers that one of them holds that which is above the fracture with his hands; and the other holds the pus below of it; and whichever of them extends to their part.
And if you want that the extension be more strong, then you strengthen over the fracture with a bandage and over that with another bandage, and whichever of the two ministers extend to their two parts.

And place the cloth and strengthen over the support the arm so that the arm is in a place of stars, if no prohibits this any cause of the broken bone.
And then use of stars.

And then strengthen over the stars with the bandage which I have said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage over the place of the fracture be pus tight. And as much as is elongated the fracture be the bandage lesser.
And therefore if you see the situation of the stars and the bandage as we have said in the hour in which you restore the limb to its situation, then you do.
And if you fear abscess, then you loosen the bandage and the stars within the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the bandage in all parts for three days so that no happens in the place proximate or inflammation; or is prohibited the food to arrive at the limb by disposition of superfluity of bandage; and therefore rectify all that according to what we have said.

And place the food of the patient according to the order which we have before said, in such a way that the food be subtle in the beginning while it is begun to strengthen; and then cover that the food of him be thickened.
Because of custom of the support and of the covering of it is that they be strengthened in forty days; and then cover that they be untied and be administered bath and be medicated with ointments those which rectify that.
And if the fracture is laid raw, then you do not untie of that the bandage and the stars within a day or within two months.
Chapter .xii.
Of the restoration of fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two forearms; of which one is small, and it is that which lies the finger towards, and the other is large and it is that which is placed below the little finger, of the part more towards.
And strong they is broken the major forearm alone, or the small, and strong they are broken together.
And when is broken the small forearm above, then the restoration of it is lighter, and the healing of it is lighter.
And when is broken the forearm of it below the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse of that is when they are broken the two bones together.
Therefore if the bone which is broken is the small forearm above, then it is convenient to the physician, he must the restoration of it, that place little extension with ease while that be wrapped.
But if the large forearm is that which is broken, then cover that place extension more vehement.
And if the two forearms are together broken, then cover that you place the extrusion more strong.

And then the physician wraps the bones while reducing that according to the best figure that he can.
And if in the fracture of the bones are splinters, then you pierce to reduce all piece in its place with your virtue.
And if appear in it splinter separated that pierces the bone and is not to your hope in the restoration of it, then you finish upon it and remove that according to the manner which we have said above.

And let the limb not be constricted until it rests, and then return the structure.
And if the structure is already softened and the bone is already remaugut and seems like those, then rectify all that with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited from reaching the limb by superfluity of structure, for then cover that you soften that a little and let it for a few days until it runs to that nourishment, and then strengthen that.
But if the patient does not happen any of those causes that we have said, then cover that it not be dislocated within about twenty days or around those.

And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or rather it is restored in twenty-eight days, and all that is according to the disposition of the complexities and the dispositions of the virtue.
